   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">New Year's Day</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub">
     <div id="mw-fr-revisiontag" class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint">

       Holiday that celebrates the new year every 1 January
      </div>

       For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_Day_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="New Year's Day (disambiguation)">New Year's Day (disambiguation)</a>. For the day itself, unrelated to celebration of the New Year, see <a href="/wiki/January_1" title="January 1">January 1</a>.

      <p><b>New Year's Day</b>, also simply called <b>New Year</b> or <b>New Year's</b>, is observed on <a href="/wiki/January_1" title="January 1">1 January</a>, the first day of the year on the modern <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>. Whilst most <a href="/wiki/Solar_calendar" title="Solar calendar">solar calendars</a> (like the Gregorian and Julian) begin the year regularly at or near the <a href="/wiki/December_solstice" title="December solstice">northern winter solstice</a>, the start of the new year in cultures that observe a <a href="/wiki/Lunisolar" class="mw-redirect" title="Lunisolar">lunisolar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">lunar calendar</a> (such as the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_New_Year" title="Chinese New Year">Chinese New Year</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_New_Year" title="Islamic New Year">Islamic New Year</a>) happen at less fixed points relative to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_year" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar year">solar year</a>. </p>
      <p>In pre-Christian Rome under the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>, the day was dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Janus" title="Janus">Janus</a>, god of gateways and beginnings, for whom <a href="/wiki/January" title="January">January</a> is also named. From Roman times until the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Eighteenth_century" class="mw-redirect" title="Eighteenth century">eighteenth century</a>, the new year was celebrated at various stages and in various parts of <a href="/wiki/Christian_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Europe">Christian Europe</a> on 25 December, on 1 March, on 25 March and on the <a href="/wiki/Computus" class="mw-redirect" title="Computus">movable feast</a> of <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sizes_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sizes-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bond_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bond-3">[3]</a></sup> </p>
      <p> In the present day, with most countries now using the Gregorian calendar as their <a href="/wiki/Civil_calendar" title="Civil calendar">civil calendar</a>, 1 January according to that calendar is among the most celebrated public <a href="/wiki/Holiday" title="Holiday">holidays</a> in the world, often observed with <a href="/wiki/Fireworks" title="Fireworks">fireworks</a> at the stroke of <a href="/wiki/Midnight" title="Midnight">midnight</a> as the <a href="/wiki/New_year" class="mw-redirect" title="New year">new year</a> starts in each time zone. Other global New Year's Day traditions include making <a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_resolution" title="New Year's resolution">New Year's resolutions</a> and calling one's friends and family.<sup id="cite_ref-Mehra2006_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mehra2006-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
      <div class="thumb tright">

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The ancient <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_calendar" title="Babylonian calendar">Babylonian calendar</a> was lunisolar, and around the year 2000<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>BC<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> began observing a spring festival and the new year during the moon of <a href="/wiki/Nisan" title="Nisan">Nisan</a>, around the time of the <a href="/wiki/Vernal_equinox_(Northern_Hemisphere)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vernal equinox (Northern Hemisphere)">vernal equinox</a>, in mid-March. The early Roman calendar designated 1 March as the first day of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The calendar had just 10 months, beginning with March. That the new year once began with the month of March is still reflected in some of the names of the months. September through to December, the ninth through to the twelfth months of the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>, were originally positioned as the seventh through to the tenth months. (<i>Septem</i> is <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for "seven"; <i>octo</i>, "eight"; <i>novem</i>, "nine"; and <i>decem</i>, "ten".) <a href="/wiki/Roman_legend" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman legend">Roman legend</a> usually credited their second <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Rome" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Rome">king</a> <a href="/wiki/Numa_Pompilius" title="Numa Pompilius">Numa</a> with the establishment of the two new months of <a href="/wiki/Ianuarius" title="Ianuarius">Ianuarius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Februarius" title="Februarius">Februarius</a>. These were first placed at the end of the year, but at some point came to be considered the first two months instead.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The January <a href="/wiki/Kalends" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalends">kalend</a> (Latin: <i lang="la"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/kalendae#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:kalendae">Kalendae</a></i> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Ianuarius#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:Ianuarius">Ianuariae</a>), the start of the month of January, came to be celebrated as the new year at some point after it became the day for the inaugurating new <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consuls</a> in 153<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>BC. Romans had long dated their years by <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_consuls" title="List of Roman consuls">these consulships</a>, rather than sequentially, and making the kalends of January start the new year aligned this dating. Still, private and religious celebrations around the March new year continued for some time and there is no consensus on the question of the timing for 1 January's new status.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Once it became the new year, however, it became a time for family gatherings and celebrations. A series of disasters, notably including the <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_Lepidus" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolt of Lepidus">failed rebellion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aemilius_Lepidus_(consul_78_BC)" title="Marcus Aemilius Lepidus (consul 78 BC)">M. Aemilius Lepidus</a> in 78<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>BC, established a superstition against allowing Rome's <a href="/wiki/Nundinae" title="Nundinae">market days</a> to fall on the <a href="/wiki/Kalends" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalends">kalends</a> of January and the <a href="/wiki/Pontifex_maximus" title="Pontifex maximus">pontiffs</a> employed <a href="/wiki/Intercalation_(calendar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Intercalation (calendar)">intercalation</a> to avoid its occurrence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacrobius[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerLRomanTextsMacrobiusSaturnalia1html13_Book_I,_Ch._xiii,_§17]_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrobius[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerLRomanTextsMacrobiusSaturnalia1html13_Book_I,_Ch._xiii,_§17]-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaster2011163_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaster2011163-9">[9]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span id="New_Year.27s_Day_in_the_Julian_and_Gregorian_calendars"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Year's_Day_in_the_Julian_and_Gregorian_calendars">New Year's Day in the Julian and Gregorian calendars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: New Year's Day in the Julian and Gregorian calendars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span id="Date_of_New_Year.27s_Day"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Date_of_New_Year's_Day">Date of New Year's Day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Date of New Year's Day">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Menologion_of_Basil_047.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Menologion_of_Basil_047.jpg/200px-Menologion_of_Basil_047.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Menologion_of_Basil_047.jpg/300px-Menologion_of_Basil_047.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Menologion_of_Basil_047.jpg/400px-Menologion_of_Basil_047.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2244" data-file-height="1504"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Menologion_of_Basil_047.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>In <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>, 1 January traditionally marks the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Circumcision_of_Christ" title="Feast of the Circumcision of Christ">Feast of the Circumcision of Christ</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Julian calendar, proposed by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ab_urbe_condita" title="Ab urbe condita">AUC</a><span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>708 (46<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span><a href="/wiki/Before_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Before Christ">BC</a>), was a reform of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_calendar" title="Roman calendar">Roman calendar</a>. It took effect on <span class="nowrap">1 January <a href="/wiki/Ab_urbe_condita" title="Ab urbe condita">AUC</a><span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>709 (45<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span><a href="/wiki/Before_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Before Christ">BC</a>)</span>, by edict. The calendar became the predominant calendar in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> and subsequently most of the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a> for more than 1,600 years. The Roman calendar began the year on 1 January, and this remained the start of the year after the Julian reform. However, even after local calendars were aligned to the Julian calendar, they started the new year on different dates. The Alexandrian calendar in Egypt started on 29 August (30 August after an Alexandrian leap year). Several local provincial calendars were aligned to start on the birthday of the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>, 23 September. The <a href="/wiki/Indiction" title="Indiction">indiction</a> caused the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> year, which used the Julian calendar, to begin on 1 September; this date is still used in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> for the beginning of the liturgical year. </p>
      <p>At various times and in various places throughout mediaeval <a href="/wiki/Christian_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Europe">Christian Europe</a>, the new year was celebrated on 25 December in honour of <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">the birth of Jesus</a>; 1 March in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Calendar">old Roman style</a>; 25 March in honour of <a href="/wiki/Lady_Day" title="Lady Day">Lady Day</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Annunciation" title="Feast of the Annunciation">Feast of the Annunciation</a>, the date of the <a href="/wiki/Conception_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conception (biology)">conception</a> of Jesus); and on the <a href="/wiki/Computus" class="mw-redirect" title="Computus">movable feast</a> of <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sizes_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sizes-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bond_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bond-3">[3]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Most nations of Western Europe officially adopted 1 January as New Year's Day somewhat before they <a href="/wiki/Adoption_of_the_Gregorian_calendar" title="Adoption of the Gregorian calendar">adopted the Gregorian calendar</a>. France changed to 1 January from 1564, most of Germany did so from 1544, the Netherlands from 1556 or 1573 according to sect, Italy (<a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Unification of Italy">not being united</a>) did so on a variety of dates, Spain and Portugal from 1556, Sweden, Norway and Denmark from 1599, Scotland from 1600, and Russia from 1725.<sup id="cite_ref-Sizes_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sizes-2">[2]</a></sup> England, Wales, Ireland and Britain's American colonies did so from 1752: until then, the first day of the new year was Lady Day, on 25 March.<sup id="cite_ref-Sizes_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sizes-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bond_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bond-3">[3]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_observance">Christian observance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Christian observance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>As a date in the Christian calendar, New Year's Day liturgically marked the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Circumcision_of_Christ" title="Feast of the Circumcision of Christ">Feast of the Naming and Circumcision of Jesus</a>, which is still observed as such in the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran Church">Lutheran Church</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> and by the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> (Julian calendar, see <a href="#Continuing_use_of_the_Julian_calendar">below</a>). The Roman Catholic Church celebrates on this day the <a href="/wiki/Solemnity_of_Mary,_Mother_of_God" title="Solemnity of Mary, Mother of God">Solemnity of Mary, Mother of God</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gift_giving">Gift giving</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Gift giving">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Among the 7th-century <a href="/wiki/Pagan" class="mw-redirect" title="Pagan">pagans</a> of <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, it was the custom to exchange gifts at the <a href="/wiki/Winter_solstice" title="Winter solstice">winter solstice</a>. This custom was deplored by <a href="/wiki/Saint_Eligius" title="Saint Eligius">Saint Eligius</a> (died 659 or 660), who warned the Flemish and Dutch: "(Do not) make vetulas, [little figures of the Old Woman], little deer or iotticos or set tables [for the house-elf, compare <a href="/wiki/Puck_(mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Puck (mythology)">Puck</a>] at night or exchange New Year gifts or supply superfluous drinks [another <a href="/wiki/Yule" title="Yule">Yule</a> custom]."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> However, on the date that European Christians celebrated the Feast of the Circumcision, they exchanged Christmas presents because the feast fell within the 12 days of the <a href="/wiki/Christmastide" title="Christmastide">Christmas season</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">Western Christian</a> liturgical <a href="/wiki/Calendar" title="Calendar">calendar</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes2008_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes2008-13">[13]</a></sup> The custom of exchanging Christmas gifts in a Christian context is traced back to the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Magi" title="Biblical Magi">Biblical Magi</a> who gave gifts to the <a href="/wiki/Child_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Child Jesus">Child Jesus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Collins2010_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collins2010-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Berking1999_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berking1999-15">[15]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Tudor_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor England">Tudor England</a>, 1 January (as the Feast of the Circumcision, not New Year's Day), along with <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="Christmas Day">Christmas Day</a> and <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Night_(holiday)" title="Twelfth Night (holiday)">Twelfth Night</a>, was celebrated as one of three main festivities among the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Days_of_Christmas" title="Twelve Days of Christmas">twelve days</a> of <a href="/wiki/Christmastide" title="Christmastide">Christmastide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sim2011_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sim2011-16">[16]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continuing_use_of_the_Julian_calendar">Continuing use of the Julian calendar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Continuing use of the Julian calendar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <table class="wikitable floatright"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <th>Calendar</th> 
         <th>Today </th>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <th>Gregorian </th> 
         <td>25 April 2021 </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <th>Julian </th> 
         <td>12 April 2021 </td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <ul>
       <li>Most (<a href="/wiki/Revised_Julian_calendar" title="Revised Julian calendar">though not all</a>) Eastern Orthodox Churches continue to use the Julian calendar which, because it adds a leap day every four years without exception, gives dates that are thirteen days behind their Gregorian equivalent. So, whilst <a href="/wiki/January_1_(Eastern_Orthodox_liturgics)" title="January 1 (Eastern Orthodox liturgics)">1 January according to the Orthodox liturgy</a> is still New Year's Day in Orthodoxy, it is the same day as 14 January Gregorian.</li> 
       <li>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> formally discarded the Julian calendar from 1752, by which time an eleven day difference between the calendars needed to be deleted. There was some <a href="/wiki/Calendar_(New_Style)_Act_1750#Religious_dissent" title="Calendar (New Style) Act 1750">religious dissent</a> regarding feast days being moved, especially Christmas Day (see <a href="/wiki/Old_Christmas" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Christmas">Old Christmas</a>) and isolated communities continued the old reckoning to a greater or lesser extent. 1800 and 1900 were leap years in the Julian calendar but not in the Gregorian, so the difference increased to twelve then thirteen days. 2000 was a leap year in both calendars. 
        <ul>
         <li>In the <a href="/wiki/Gwaun_Valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Gwaun Valley">Gwaun Valley</a> in Wales, <a href="/wiki/Gwaun_Valley#New_Year_celebrations" class="mw-redirect" title="Gwaun Valley">the new year is celebrated on 13 January</a>, still based on the <a href="/wiki/Nineteenth_century" class="mw-redirect" title="Nineteenth century">nineteenth century</a> difference in the calendars.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></li> 
         <li><a href="/wiki/Foula" title="Foula">Foula</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> islands celebrates <a href="/wiki/Yule" title="Yule">Yule</a> ('Old Christmas' rather than the <a href="/wiki/December_solstice" title="December solstice">December solstice</a>) on 6 January and Newerday on 13 January.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Again, both dates reflect the nineteenth century reckoning and were not moved again in 1900.</li>
        </ul></li> 
       <li>The <a href="/wiki/Old_New_Year" title="Old New Year">Old New Year</a> in <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> is commonly called the <b>Serbian New Year</b> (Српска Нова година / Srpska Nova Godina),<sup id="cite_ref-insight_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insight-19">[19]</a></sup> celebrated on 14 January (Gregorian calendar) as the start of the New Year, being the same day as 1 January by the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Orthodox_Church" title="Serbian Orthodox Church">Serbian Orthodox Church</a>, with traditional adherence in Serbia (including <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, celebrates its feasts and holidays according to the Julian calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-insight_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insight-19">[19]</a></sup> A part of the population celebrates <i>Serbian New Year</i> in a similar way as the New Year on 1 January (Gregorian) is celebrated elsewhere. This time, usually one concert is organised in front of either City Hall or the National Parliament (in <a href="/wiki/Belgrade" title="Belgrade">Belgrade</a>), while <a href="/wiki/Fireworks" title="Fireworks">fireworks</a> are prepared by the Serbian Orthodox Church and fired from the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Saint_Sava" title="Church of Saint Sava">Church of Saint Sava</a>, where people also gather. Other cities also organise such celebrations. Restaurants, clubs, cafes, and hotels are usually fully booked and organise New Year's Day celebrations with food and live music.<sup id="cite_ref-insight_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-insight-19">[19]</a></sup></li> 
       <li>Since 1918, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> uses the Gregorian calendar as its <a href="/wiki/Civil_calendar" title="Civil calendar">civil calendar</a> but the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> continues to use the Julian or '<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">old style</a>' calendar. Accordingly, 14<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>January [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a><span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>1<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>January] is a <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Russia#Popular_holidays_which_are_not_public_holidays" title="Public holidays in Russia">non-public holiday</a> in Russia.</li>
      </ul> 
      <h2><span id="New_Year.27s_Days_in_other_calendars"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Year's_Days_in_other_calendars">New Year's Days in other calendars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: New Year's Days in other calendars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>

      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Countries_that_do_not_celebrate_New_Year%E2%80%99s_Day_on_1st_January.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Countries_that_do_not_celebrate_New_Year%E2%80%99s_Day_on_1st_January.png/220px-Countries_that_do_not_celebrate_New_Year%E2%80%99s_Day_on_1st_January.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Countries_that_do_not_celebrate_New_Year%E2%80%99s_Day_on_1st_January.png/330px-Countries_that_do_not_celebrate_New_Year%E2%80%99s_Day_on_1st_January.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Countries_that_do_not_celebrate_New_Year%E2%80%99s_Day_on_1st_January.png/440px-Countries_that_do_not_celebrate_New_Year%E2%80%99s_Day_on_1st_January.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="882"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Countries_that_do_not_celebrate_New_Year%E2%80%99s_Day_on_1st_January.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Countries where the main celebrations of the New Year are other day than on 1 January
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In cultures that traditionally or currently use calendars other than the Gregorian, New Year's Day is often also an important celebration. Some countries concurrently use Gregorian and another calendar. New Year's Day in the alternative calendar attracts alternative celebrations of that new year: </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="African">African</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: African">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <ul>
       <li><a href="/wiki/Nayrouz" title="Nayrouz">Nayrouz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enkutatash" title="Enkutatash">Enkutatash</a> are the New Year's Days of the Coptic Egyptians and the Ethiopians, respectively. Between 1900 and 2100, both occur on 11 September in most years and on 12 September in the years before Gregorian <a href="/wiki/Leap_year" title="Leap year">leap years</a>. They preserve the legacy of the ancient Egyptian new year Wepet Renpet, which originally marked the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> <a href="/wiki/Flooding_of_the_Nile" title="Flooding of the Nile">flood</a> but which <a href="/wiki/Sothic_cycle" title="Sothic cycle">wandered through the seasons</a> until the introduction of leap years to the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_calendar" title="Egyptian calendar">traditional calendar</a> by <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> in 30-20<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>BC. In Ethiopia, the new year is held to mark the end of the summer <a href="/wiki/Rainy_season" class="mw-redirect" title="Rainy season">rainy season</a>.</li> 
       <li>The <a href="/wiki/Odunde_Festival" title="Odunde Festival">Odunde Festival</a> is also called the <b>African New Year</b> is celebrated in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> on the second Sunday of June. While the name was based on the <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_language" title="Yoruba language">Yoruba</a> African culture, its celebration marks the largest African celebration in the world, which more or less was started by a local tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></li> 
       <li>The <a href="/wiki/Sotho_people" title="Sotho people">Sotho people</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> celebrate <b>Selemo sa Basotho</b> on 1 August during the end of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>'s winter. This is based on the <a href="/wiki/Sotho_calendar" title="Sotho calendar">Sotho calendar</a>, and includes observances such as "Mokete wa lewa", a celebration which follows the harvest.</li>
      </ul> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asian">East Asian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: East Asian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <ul>
       <li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_New_Year" title="Chinese New Year">Chinese New Year</a> is celebrated in some countries around East Asia, including China, and South-east Asia, including Singapore. It is the first day of the lunar calendar and is corrected for the solar every three years. The holiday normally falls between 20 January and 20 February.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The holiday is celebrated with food, families, lucky money (usually in a <a href="/wiki/Red_envelope" title="Red envelope">red envelope</a>), and many other red things for good luck. Lion and dragon dances, drums, fireworks, firecrackers, and other types of entertainment fill the streets on this day. 1 January is also a legal holiday in China, and people will also celebrate the Gregorian New Year in this day, but it is not as grand as the traditional Chinese New Year.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Japanese_New_Year" title="Japanese New Year">Japanese New Year</a> is celebrated on 1 January because the Gregorian calendar is now used instead of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calendar" title="Chinese calendar">Chinese calendar</a>.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Korean_New_Year" title="Korean New Year">Korean New Year</a> is celebrated on the first day of the solar calendar and lunar calendar respectively in <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>. The first day of the lunar calendar, called Seollal (설날), is a big national holiday with the Korean thanksgiving Day, called Chuseok(추석).<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> South Koreans also celebrate solar New Year's Day on 1 January each year, following the Gregorian Calendar. New Year's Day is also a national holiday, so people have the day off while they have a minimum of three days off for Lunar New Year. Koreans now consider solar New Year’s Day as the first day of the year, while the first day of the lunar calendar is considered a traditional holiday. Koreans celebrate New Year's Day by preparing food for their ancestors' spirits, visiting ancestors' graves, and playing Korean games such as <a href="/wiki/Yunnori" class="mw-redirect" title="Yunnori">Yunnori</a> (윷놀이) with families. Young children show respect to their parents, grandparents, relatives, and other elders by bowing down in a traditional way and are given good wishes and some money by the elders. Families also enjoy the New Year by counting down to midnight on New Year's Eve on 31 December.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Koreans</a> celebrate the New Year's Day holiday on the first day of the solar calendar, 1 January. Solar New Year’s Day, called "Seollal(설날)", is a big holiday in North Korea, while they take a day off on the first day of the lunar calendar. The first day of the lunar calendar is regarded as a day for relaxation. North Koreans consider the first day of the solar calendar to be even more important.</li>
      </ul> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asian">Southeast Asian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Southeast Asian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/w/index.php?title=South_and_Southeast_Asian_solar_Year&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="South and Southeast Asian solar Year (page does not exist)">South and Southeast Asian solar Year</a>
      </div> 
      <ul>
       <li><a href="/wiki/Cambodian_New_Year" title="Cambodian New Year">Cambodian New Year</a> (Chaul Chnam Thmey) is celebrated on 13 April or 14 April. There are three days for the Khmer New Year: the first day is called "Moha Songkran", the second is called "Virak Wanabat" and the final day is called "Virak Loeurng Sak". During these periods, Cambodians often go to the pagoda or play traditional games. Phnom Penh is usually quiet during Khmer New Year as most of the Cambodians prefer spending it at their respective hometowns.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Thai_people" title="Thai people">Thai</a> New Year is celebrated on 13 April or 14 April and is called <a href="/wiki/Songkran" title="Songkran">Songkran</a> in the local language. People usually come out to splash water on one another. The throwing of water originated as a blessing. By capturing the water after it had been poured over the Buddhas for cleansing, this "blessed" water is gently poured on the shoulder of elders and family for good fortune.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Thingyan" title="Thingyan">Thingyan</a>, Burmese new year's celebrations, typically begin on 13 April but the actual New Year's day falls on 17 April in the 21st century. The day has slowly drifted over the centuries. In the 20th century, the day fell on 15 or 16 April while in the 17th century, it fell on 9 or 10 April.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/T%E1%BA%BFt" title="Tết">Vietnamese New Year</a> (Tết Nguyên Đán or Tết), more commonly known by its shortened name Tết or "Vietnamese Lunar New Year", is the most important and popular holiday and festival in Vietnam, the holiday normally falls between 20 January and 20 February. It is the Vietnamese New Year marking the arrival of spring based on the Chinese calendar, a lunisolar calendar. The name Tết Nguyên Đán is Sino-Vietnamese for Feast of the First Morning, derived from the Hán nôm characters 節 元 旦.</li>
      </ul> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Asian">South Asian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: South Asian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <ul>
       <li><a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> in India celebrate 1 January as the New Year according to the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>. <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Christians">Catholic Christians</a> also celebrate 1 January as The <a href="/wiki/Solemnity_of_Mary,_the_Mother_of_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Solemnity of Mary, the Mother of God">Solemnity of Mary, the Mother of God</a>, the liturgical feast of the <a href="/wiki/Blessed_Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Blessed Virgin Mary">Blessed Virgin Mary</a>.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Diwali#New_Year_celebrations" title="Diwali">Diwali</a> related New Year's celebrations include Marwari new year and Gujarati new year.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Indian_New_Year%27s_days" title="Indian New Year's days">Indian New Year's days</a> has several variations depending on the region and is based on the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_calendar" title="Hindu calendar">Hindu calendar</a>.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> In Hinduism, different regional cultures celebrate the new year at different times of the year. In Assam, Bengal, Kerala, Nepal, Odisha, Punjab, Telangana, Andra Pradesh and Tamil Nadu households celebrate the new year when the Sun enters <a href="/wiki/Aries_(astrology)" title="Aries (astrology)">Aries</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_calendar" title="Hindu calendar">Hindu calendar</a>. This is normally on 14 April or 15 April, depending on the leap year. Elsewhere in northern/central India, the <a href="/wiki/Vikram_Samvat" title="Vikram Samvat">Vikram Samvat</a> calendar is followed. According to that, the new year day is the first day of the Chaitra Month, also known as Chaitra Shukla Pratipada or <a href="/wiki/Gudi_Padwa" title="Gudi Padwa">Gudi Padwa</a>. This is basically the first month of the Hindu calendar, the first Shukla paksha (fortnight) and the first day. This normally comes around 23–24 March, mostly around the Spring Equinox in Gregorian Calendar. The new year is celebrated by paying respect to elders in the family and by seeking their blessings. They also exchange tokens of good wishes for a healthy and prosperous year ahead.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Malayalam" title="Malayalam">Malayalam</a> New Year (Puthuvarsham) is celebrated either on the first day of the month of Medam in mid-April which is known as Vishu, or the first day of the month of <a href="/wiki/Chingam" class="mw-redirect" title="Chingam">Chingam</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Malayalam_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Malayalam Calendar">Malayalam Calendar</a> in mid-August according to another reckoning. Unlike most other calendar systems in India, the New Year's Day on the <a href="/wiki/Malayalam_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Malayalam Calendar">Malayalam Calendar</a> is not based on any astronomical event. It is just the first day of the first of the 12 months on the <a href="/wiki/Malayalam_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Malayalam Calendar">Malayalam Calendar</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Malayalam_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Malayalam Calendar">Malayalam Calendar</a> (called Kollavarsham) originated in 825 AD, based on general agreement among scholars, with the re-opening of the city of <a href="/wiki/Kollam" title="Kollam">Kollam</a> (on <a href="/wiki/Malabar_Coast" title="Malabar Coast">Malabar Coast</a>), which had been destroyed by a <a href="/wiki/Natural_disaster" title="Natural disaster">natural disaster</a>.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Nepal_Sambat" title="Nepal Sambat">Nepal Sambat</a> is the Nepalese New Year celebration.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Pahela_Baishakh" title="Pahela Baishakh">Pahela Baishakh</a> or Bangla Nabobarsho is the first day of the Bengali Calendar. It is celebrated on 14 April as a national holiday in Bangladesh, and on 14 or 15 April in the Indian states of West Bengal, Tripura and part of Assam by people of Bengali heritage, irrespective of their religious faith.</li> 
       <li>The Sikh New Year is celebrated as per the <a href="/wiki/Nanakshahi_calendar" title="Nanakshahi calendar">Nanakshahi calendar</a>. The epoch of this calendar is the birth of the first <a href="/wiki/The_Sikh_Gurus" class="mw-redirect" title="The Sikh Gurus">Sikh Guru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> in 1469. New Year's Day falls annually on what is 14 March in the Gregorian Western calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Sinhalese_New_Year" title="Sinhalese New Year">Sinhalese New Year</a> is celebrated in Sri Lankan culture predominantly by the Sri Lankan Sinhalese, while the <a href="/wiki/Tamil_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Tamil people">Tamil</a> New Year on the same day is celebrated by Sri Lankan Tamils. The Sinhalese New Year (<i>aluth avurudda</i>), marks the end of the harvest season, by the month of Bak (April) between 13 and 14 April. There is an astrologically generated time gap between the passing year and the New Year, which is based on the passing of the sun from the Meena Rashiya (House of Pisces) to the Mesha Rashiya (House of Aries) in the celestial sphere. The astrological time difference between the New Year and the passing year (<i>nonagathe</i>) is celebrated with several Buddhist rituals and customs that are to be concentrated on, which are exclusive of all types of 'work'. After Buddhist rituals and traditions are attended to, Sinhala and Tamil New Year-based social gatherings and festive parties with the aid of firecrackers, and fireworks would be organised. The exchange of gifts, cleanliness, the lighting of the oil lamp, making <i>kiribath</i> (milk rice), and even the Asian Koel are significant aspects of the Sinhalese New Year.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Tamil_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Tamil people">Tamil</a> New Year (<a href="/wiki/Puthandu" title="Puthandu">Puthandu</a>) is celebrated on 13 April or 14 April. Traditionally, it is celebrated as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiththirai_Thirunaal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Chiththirai Thirunaal (page does not exist)">Chiththirai Thirunaal</a> in parts of Tamil Nadu to mark the event of the Sun entering Aries. <a href="/wiki/Panchangam" title="Panchangam">Panchangam</a> (<a href="/wiki/Almanac" title="Almanac">almanac</a>), is read in temples to mark the start of the Year.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Telugu_people" title="Telugu people">Telugu</a> New Year (<a href="/wiki/Ugadi" title="Ugadi">Ugadi</a>), <a href="/wiki/Kannada" title="Kannada">Kannada</a> New Year (<a href="/wiki/Yugadi" class="mw-redirect" title="Yugadi">Yugadi</a>) is celebrated in March (generally), April (occasionally). Traditionally, it is celebrated as Chaitram <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chaitra_Shuddha_Padyami&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Chaitra Shuddha Padyami (page does not exist)">Chaitra Shuddha Padyami</a> in parts of Andhra Pradesh, Telangana and Karnataka to mark the event of New Year's Day for the people of the Deccan region of India. It falls on a different day every year because the Hindu calendar is a lunisolar calendar. The Saka calendar begins with the month of Chaitra (March–April) and Ugadi/Yugadi marks the first day of the new year. Chaitra is the first month in Panchanga which is the Indian calendar. <a href="/wiki/Panchangam" title="Panchangam">Panchangam</a> (<a href="/wiki/Almanac" title="Almanac">almanac</a>), is read in temples to mark the start of the Year.</li>
      </ul> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Eastern">Middle Eastern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Middle Eastern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <ul>
       <li><b>Hijri New Year</b> in the <a href="/wiki/Islamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> culture is also known as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_new_year&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Islamic new year (page does not exist)">Islamic new year</a> (Arabic: رأس السنة الهجرية Ras as-Sanah al-Hijriyah) is the day that marks the beginning of a new Islamic calendar year. New Year moves from year to year because the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic calendar</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">lunar calendar</a>. The first day of the year is observed on the first day of Muharram, the first month in the Islamic calendar.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Nowruz" title="Nowruz">Nowruz</a> also known as Persian and Kurdish New Year marks the first day of spring and the beginning of the year in the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian calendar">Iranian calendar</a>. It is celebrated on the day of the astronomical vernal equinox, which usually occurs on 21 March or the previous/following day depending on where it is observed. Nowruz has been celebrated for over 3,000 years by the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">related cultural continent</a>. The holiday is also celebrated and observed by many parts of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Northwestern China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a> and some groups in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. As well as being a <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a> holiday and having significance amongst the Zoroastrian ancestors of modern Iranians, the same time is celebrated in the Indian sub-continent as the new year. The moment the Sun crosses the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_equator" title="Celestial equator">celestial equator</a> and equalises night and day is calculated exactly every year and <a href="/wiki/Iranian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian people">Iranian</a> families gather together to observe the rituals.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Rosh_Hashanah" title="Rosh Hashanah">Rosh Hashanah</a>, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_new_year&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jewish new year (page does not exist)">Jewish new year</a>, is celebrated by Jews in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and throughout the world. The date is the new moon of <a href="/wiki/Tishrei" title="Tishrei">Tishrei</a>, which is the seventh month counting from <a href="/wiki/Nisan" title="Nisan">Nisan</a>, the first month of Spring. It always falls during September or October. The holiday is celebrated by blasting of <a href="/wiki/Shofar" title="Shofar">shofar</a> trumpets, to signify it as a day of judgement, by <a href="/wiki/Selichot" title="Selichot">prayers of penitence</a>, by readings from the law and prophets, and by special meals. The night of 31 December/1 January, the New Year according to the Gregorian calendar, is also celebrated widely in Israel and is referred to as <a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_Eve#Israel" title="New Year's Eve">Sylvester</a> or the civil new year.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
      </ul> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_and_modern_celebrations_and_customs">Traditional and modern celebrations and customs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Traditional and modern celebrations and customs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <h3><span id="New_Year.27s_Eve"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Year's_Eve">New Year's Eve</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: New Year's Eve">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_Eve" title="New Year's Eve">New Year's Eve</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:OperaSydney-Fuegos2006-342289398.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/OperaSydney-Fuegos2006-342289398.jpg/330px-OperaSydney-Fuegos2006-342289398.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/OperaSydney-Fuegos2006-342289398.jpg/495px-OperaSydney-Fuegos2006-342289398.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/OperaSydney-Fuegos2006-342289398.jpg/660px-OperaSydney-Fuegos2006-342289398.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="539"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:OperaSydney-Fuegos2006-342289398.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a> contributes to some of the major New Year celebrations each year.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The first of January represents the fresh start of a new year after a period of remembrance of the passing year, including on radio, television, and in newspapers, which starts in early December in countries around the world. Publications have year-end articles that review the changes during the previous year. In some cases, publications may set their entire year work alight in the hope that the smoke emitted from the flame brings new life to the company. There are also articles on planned or expected changes in the coming year. </p>
      <p>This day is traditionally a <a href="/wiki/Religious_festival" title="Religious festival">religious feast</a>, but since <a href="/wiki/1900%E2%80%931909" class="mw-redirect" title="1900–1909">the 1900s</a> has also become an occasion to celebrate the night of 31 December—<a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_Eve" title="New Year's Eve">New Year's Eve</a>—with parties, public celebrations (often involving <a href="/wiki/Firework" class="mw-redirect" title="Firework">fireworks</a> shows) and other traditions focused on the impending arrival of <a href="/wiki/Midnight" title="Midnight">midnight</a> and the new year. <a href="/wiki/Watchnight_service" title="Watchnight service">Watchnight services</a> are also still observed by many.<sup id="cite_ref-BOW_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOW-25">[25]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="New_Year.27s_Day"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Year's_Day">New Year's Day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: New Year's Day">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The celebrations and activities held worldwide on 1 January as part of New Year's Day commonly include the following: </p> 
      <ul>
       <li>Several major <a href="/wiki/Parade" title="Parade">parades</a> are held on New Year's Day, including the <a href="/wiki/London%27s_New_Year%27s_Day_Parade" title="London's New Year's Day Parade">London's New Year's Day Parade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pasadena,_California" title="Pasadena, California">Pasadena's</a> <a href="/wiki/Tournament_of_Roses_Parade" class="mw-redirect" title="Tournament of Roses Parade">Tournament of Roses Parade</a> (also known as the "Rose Parade"), and <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia's</a> <a href="/wiki/Mummers_Parade" title="Mummers Parade">Mummers Parade</a>. In the Bahamas, it is also associated with <a href="/wiki/Junkanoo" title="Junkanoo">Junkanoos</a>.</li> 
       <li>Beginning in the 2010s, it is also the day that <a href="/wiki/First_Day_Hikes" title="First Day Hikes">First Day Hikes</a> takes place in the <a href="/wiki/State_park#United_States_of_America" title="State park">fifty state park systems of the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></li> 
       <li>The <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Philharmonic" title="Vienna Philharmonic">Vienna Philharmonic</a> orchestra traditionally performs a <a href="/wiki/Vienna_New_Year%27s_Concert" title="Vienna New Year's Concert">New Year's concert</a> on the morning of New Year's Day.</li> 
       <li>A "<a href="/wiki/Polar_bear_plunge" title="Polar bear plunge">polar bear plunge</a>" is a common tradition in some countries, where participants gather on beaches and run into the cold water. <a href="/wiki/Polar_Bear_Clubs" class="mw-redirect" title="Polar Bear Clubs">Polar Bear Clubs</a> in many <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> cities have a tradition of holding organised plunges on New Year's Day, and they are often held to raise money for <a href="/wiki/Charitable_organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Charitable organisation">charity</a>.</li> 
       <li>In <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, New Year's Day was called <i>Lá na gCeapairí</i>, or the day of the buttered bread. A possible meaning to the consumption of buttered bread was to ward off hunger and famine in the coming year, by placing the buttered bread on the doorstep in the morning. Some traditions saw parties of young people calling from house to house to receive buttered bread and occasionally <a href="/wiki/Poit%C3%ADn" title="Poitín">Poitín</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> or to give out buttered bread in exchange for pennies.This tradition has since died out, having been popular in the 19th century, and waning in the 1930s and 1940s.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></li> 
       <li>In the United Kingdom and United States, New Year's Day is associated with several prominent sporting events: 
        <ul>
         <li>In the United States, 1 January is the traditional date for several major post-season <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">college football</a> <a href="/wiki/Bowl_games" class="mw-redirect" title="Bowl games">bowl games</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Citrus_Bowl" title="Citrus Bowl">Citrus Bowl</a> in <a href="/wiki/Orlando" class="mw-redirect" title="Orlando">Orlando</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Outback_Bowl" title="Outback Bowl">Outback Bowl</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">Tampa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rose_Bowl_Game" title="Rose Bowl Game">Rose Bowl Game</a> in Pasadena, and the <a href="/wiki/Sugar_Bowl" title="Sugar Bowl">Sugar Bowl</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>. Since 2008, the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a> has hosted an annual <a href="/wiki/NHL_outdoor_games" title="NHL outdoor games">outdoor game</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Winter_Classic" class="mw-redirect" title="Winter Classic">Winter Classic</a></i>, which rotates between different host teams annually, and usually showcases a major regional <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League_rivalries" title="National Hockey League rivalries">rivalry</a>. If New Year's Day falls on a Sunday, sporting events and associated festivities (such as the Rose Parade) traditionally held on New Year's Day are typically deferred to 2 January in defense of the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a>—which plays the final gameday of its regular season.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li> 
         <li>The <a href="/wiki/Premier_League" title="Premier League">Premier League</a> in English <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> traditionally holds a fixture of matches on New Year's Day,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> stemming from the historic tradition of games being played over the Christmas holiday period (including, just as prominently, <a href="/wiki/Boxing_Day" title="Boxing Day">Boxing Day</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></li> 
         <li>The final of the <a href="/wiki/PDC_World_Darts_Championship" title="PDC World Darts Championship">PDC World Darts Championship</a> typically falls on New Year's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li> 
         <li>The <a href="/wiki/Cheltenham_Racecourse" title="Cheltenham Racecourse">Cheltenham Racecourse</a> holds a New Year's Day fixture, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Fairlawne_Handicap_Chase" title="Fairlawne Handicap Chase">Fairlawne Handicap Chase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dipper_Novices%27_Chase" title="Dipper Novices' Chase">Dipper Novices' Chase</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Relkeel_Hurdle" title="Relkeel Hurdle">Relkeel Hurdle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
        </ul></li> 
       <li>New Year's Day is a government and bank holiday in many countries.</li>
      </ul> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Music associated with New Year's Day comes in both classical and popular genres, and there is also <a href="/wiki/Christmas_music" title="Christmas music">Christmas song</a> focus on the arrival of a new year during the <a href="/wiki/Christmas_and_holiday_season" title="Christmas and holiday season">Christmas and holiday season</a>. </p> 
      <ul>
       <li><a href="/wiki/Paul_Gerhardt" title="Paul Gerhardt">Paul Gerhardt</a> wrote the text for a hymn for the turn of the year, "<a href="/wiki/Nun_lasst_uns_gehn_und_treten" title="Nun lasst uns gehn und treten">Nun lasst uns gehn und treten</a>", first published in 1653.</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Orgelb%C3%BCchlein" title="Orgelbüchlein">Orgelbüchlein</a>, composed three chorale preludes for the new year: <i>Helft mir Gotts Güte preisen</i> ["Help me to praise God's goodness"] (BWV 613); <i>Das alte Jahr vergangen ist</i> ["The old year has passed"] (BWV 614); and <i>In dir ist freude</i> ["In you is joy"] (BWV 615).<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li> 
       <li><i>The year is gone, beyond recall</i> is a traditional Christian hymn to give thanks for the new year, dating back to 1713.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li> 
       <li>In English-speaking countries, it is traditional to sing <a href="/wiki/Auld_Lang_Syne" title="Auld Lang Syne">Auld Lang Syne</a> at midnight on New Year's.</li>
      </ul> 
      <h3><span id="New_Year.27s_Day_babies"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Year's_Day_babies">New Year's Day babies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: New Year's Day babies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>A common image used, often as an editorial cartoon, is that of an incarnation of <a href="/wiki/Father_Time" title="Father Time">Father Time</a> (or the "Old Year") wearing a sash across his chest with the previous year printed on it passing on his duties to the <a href="/wiki/Baby_New_Year" title="Baby New Year">Baby New Year</a> (or the "New Year"), an infant wearing a sash with the new year printed on it.<sup id="cite_ref-Birx2009_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birx2009-36">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Babies born on New Year's Day are commonly called <i>New Year babies</i>. Hospitals, such as the Dyersburg Regional Medical Center<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> in the US, give out prizes to the first baby born in that hospital in the new year. These prizes are often donated by local businesses. Prizes may include various baby-related items such as <a href="/wiki/Infant_formula" title="Infant formula">baby formula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blanket" title="Blanket">baby blankets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diaper" title="Diaper">diapers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gift_certificate" class="mw-redirect" title="Gift certificate">gift certificates</a> to stores which specialise in baby-related merchandise. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_celebrations_on_1_January">Other celebrations on 1 January</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Other celebrations on 1 January">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran Church">Lutheran Church</a> celebrate the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Circumcision_of_Christ" title="Feast of the Circumcision of Christ">Feast of the Circumcision of Christ</a> on 1 January, based on the belief that if Jesus was born on 25 December, then according to Hebrew tradition, his <a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">circumcision</a> would have taken place on the eighth day of his life (1 January). The Roman Catholic Church celebrates on this day the <a href="/wiki/Solemnity_of_Mary,_Mother_of_God" title="Solemnity of Mary, Mother of God">Solemnity of Mary, Mother of God</a>, which is also a <a href="/wiki/Holy_Day_of_Obligation" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Day of Obligation">Holy Day of Obligation</a>. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a> composed several <a href="/wiki/List_of_Bach_cantatas" title="List of Bach cantatas">church cantatas</a> for the double occasion: </p> 
      <ul>
       <li><a href="/wiki/Singet_dem_Herrn_ein_neues_Lied,_BWV_190" title="Singet dem Herrn ein neues Lied, BWV 190"><i>Singet dem Herrn ein neues Lied</i>, BWV 190</a>, 1 January 1724</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Jesu,_nun_sei_gepreiset,_BWV_41" title="Jesu, nun sei gepreiset, BWV 41"><i>Jesu, nun sei gepreiset</i>, BWV 41</a>, 1 January 1725</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Herr_Gott,_dich_loben_wir,_BWV_16" title="Herr Gott, dich loben wir, BWV 16"><i>Herr Gott, dich loben wir</i>, BWV 16</a>, 1 January 1726</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Gott,_wie_dein_Name,_so_ist_auch_dein_Ruhm,_BWV_171" title="Gott, wie dein Name, so ist auch dein Ruhm, BWV 171"><i>Gott, wie dein Name, so ist auch dein Ruhm</i>, BWV 171</a>, 1 January 1729(?)</li> 
       <li><a href="/wiki/Fallt_mit_Danken,_fallt_mit_Loben,_BWV_248_IV" title="Fallt mit Danken, fallt mit Loben, BWV 248 IV"><i>Fallt mit Danken, fallt mit Loben</i></a>, 1 January 1735 (<i>Christmas Oratorio</i> Part IV)</li>
      </ul>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Year%27s_Day&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

         <li id="cite_note-Forbes2008-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Forbes2008_13-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">

           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFCollins2010" class="citation book cs1">Collins, Ace (4 May 2010). <i>Stories Behind the Great Traditions of Christmas</i>. Harper Collins. p.&nbsp;88. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780310873884" title="Special:BookSources/9780310873884"><bdi>9780310873884</bdi></a>. <q>Most people today trace the practice of giving gifts on Christmas Day to the three gifts that the Magi gave to Jesus.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Stories+Behind+the+Great+Traditions+of+Christmas&amp;rft.pages=88&amp;rft.pub=Harper+Collins&amp;rft.date=2010-05-04&amp;rft.isbn=9780310873884&amp;rft.aulast=Collins&amp;rft.aufirst=Ace&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANew+Year%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-Berking1999-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Berking1999_15-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFBerking1999" class="citation book cs1">Berking, Helmuth (30 March 1999). <i>Sociology of Giving</i>. SAGE Publications. p.&nbsp;14. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780857026132" title="Special:BookSources/9780857026132"><bdi>9780857026132</bdi></a>. <q>The winter solstice was a time of festivity in every traditional culture, and the Christian Christmas probably took its place within this mythical context of the solar cult. Its core dogma of the Incarnation, however, solidly established the giving and receiving of gifts as the structural principle of that recurrent yet unique event. 'Children were given presents as the Jesus child received gifts from the magi or kings who came from afar to adore him. But in reality it was they, together with all their fellow men, who received the gift of God through man's renewed participation in the divine life' (ibid.: 61).</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Sociology+of+Giving&amp;rft.pages=14&amp;rft.pub=SAGE+Publications&amp;rft.date=1999-03-30&amp;rft.isbn=9780857026132&amp;rft.aulast=Berking&amp;rft.aufirst=Helmuth&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANew+Year%27s+Day" class="Z3988"></span></span> </li>

                <th scope="row" class="navbox-group" style="width:1%">National holidays</th>
                <td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">

                 <div>
                  Legend: 
                  <p>(federal) = federal holidays, (abbreviation) = state/territorial holidays, (religious) = religious holidays, (cultural) = holiday related to a specific <a href="/wiki/Race_and_ethnicity_in_the_United_States_Census" title="Race and ethnicity in the United States Census">racial/ethnic group</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sexual_minority" title="Sexual minority">sexual minority</a>, (week) = week-long holidays, (month) = month-long holidays, (36) = <a href="/wiki/Title_36_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 36 of the United States Code">Title 36 Observances and Ceremonies</a><br> <b>Bold</b> indicates major holidays commonly celebrated in the United States, which often represent the major celebrations of the month.<br> </p> See also: <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_holidays" title="Lists of holidays">Lists of holidays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hallmark_holiday" title="Hallmark holiday">Hallmark holidays</a>, public holidays in the <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_the_United_States" title="Public holidays in the United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/State_holidays_in_New_Jersey" title="State holidays in New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_government_holidays" title="New York State government holidays">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Public holidays in Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_the_United_States_Virgin_Islands" title="Public holidays in the United States Virgin Islands">United States Virgin Islands</a>.
